Development and test of arch bridge with materials of di’¼Ćerent sti’¼Ćness

Preface
This MasterŌĆÖs Thesis is submitted as a partial ful’¼ülment of the requirements for graduating as
a Master of Science in Civil Engineering at the Technical University of Denmark.
The project is carried out in cooperation with Abeo A/S under supervision of Professor at DTU,
Kristian D. Hertz, Ph.D. student at DTU, Philip S. Halding and Associate Professor at DTU,
Jacob W. Schmidt.
The project comprises of 35 ECTS points for each of the authors and took place from September
2014 to February 2015.
The two authors have worked in close cooperation during the entire project, where Maria was
main responsible for stress distribution and stress development during loading, while Nanna
was main responsible for material studies and scaling of test specimens.
We would like to thank our supervisors Kristian and Philip for guiding us with both theoretical
and practical understanding of the subject.
A thanks also goes to our supervisor Jacob, Ph.D student at University of Washington, Bryan
Kennedy and Project Manager at Abeo A/S, Nicki V. Petersen for inputs during our meetings
and tests on how to improve our work.
Finally we would like to thank the technical sta’¼Ćin Building 119 at DTU, for helping us with
the production of moulds and test setup and a special thanks to Christian P. Rasmussen for
helping us solve whatever problem we ran into.
Nanna Tange Bech, s092113
Maria Holst Pedersen, s093399

Abstract
Arch bridges are perfect for carrying dead load and distributed loads, but often vulnerable to
concentrated loads such as live loads from heavy vehicles. In this project, structures that are
capable of carrying such moment loads, are developed and investigated by application of a ’¼ülling
material between arch and top plate. The arch consists of SL elements connected by the pearl
chain concept both developed by the Technical University of Denmark and Abeo A/S.
In this project, scaled models were used to study the e’¼Ćect of the ’¼ülling material between arch
and top plate. Scaling theory was used to determine that a scaling factor of 1:12, in comparison
to the prototype bridge, was reasonable.
A material study was also developed in order to
obtain similar material properties to the prototype bridge. Mortar was chosen to represent
the concrete in the SL decks and top plate, while gypsum was chosen to represent the light
aggregated concrete in the SL decks and ’¼ülling material.
In order to investigate the e’¼Ćect of the ’¼ülling material a number of test models were constructed.
First a simple bridge only consisting of an arch was tested as a reference. Afterwards 4 ’¼ülled
spandrel arch bridges with post tensioned top plates were tested, where the height and sti’¼Ćness
of the ’¼ülling varied, and ’¼ünally a bridge without post tensioning in the top plate was tested.
Furthermore the theoretical bearing capacities for each of the bridges were calculated, where
it was found that increasing the height of ’¼ülling between arch and top plate resulted in an
increasing bearing capacity, while the variation of the sti’¼Ćness did not a’¼Ćect the theoretical
bearing capacity. It was also seen that the applied post tensioning in the top plate also resulted
in an increased theoretical bearing capacity. Finally the tests showed that an increase of the
sti’¼Ćness of the ’¼ülling material resulted in a higher capacity, which the increased height of the
’¼ülling also did.
During the tests of the bridges, di’¼Ćerent measuring methods were used to collect data for
displacement and strain. The ARAMIS 2D- and 3D software were used to measure displacement
and strain, where LVDTs were used to measure the displacements at di’¼Ćerent locations in
di’¼Ćerent directions. From the displacements it was seen that the bridges acted as expected, i.e.
the loaded side was moved down and the non loaded side moved upwards. It was also expected
that these displacements would result in compression in the top plate and tension in the arch
on the loaded side and opposite at the non loaded side. This was checked with the ARAMIS
software where strains were calculated by manually measuring point-to-point distances. From
the strain calculations it was seen that the locations for tension- and compression stresses
occurred relatively random. The strains in the ’¼ülling were also measured from ARAMIS by
performing manual calculations in di’¼Ćerent directions, this also resulted in a relatively random
distribution. The strain plots from ARAMIS were very inaccurate and were therefore only used
to see where large strains and cracks occurred during the tests. In general it was clear that the
joint between arch and ’¼ülling, as well as the joint between ’¼ülling and top plate were critical
areas for all cases. It was also illustrating that the cracks occurred between the 1/4 point and
the 3/4 point, independent of the changed parameters.
Keywords:
Filled spandrel arch bridge, SL deck, Pearl-chain concept, Post tensioning, Scaled modelling,
ARAMIS.

kan optage disse momentp╦Üavirkninger, udviklet og unders├Ėgt ved anvendelse af et fyldmateri-

princippet, som begge er udviklet af Danmarks Tekniske Universitet og Abeo A/S.
I dette projekt blev e’¼Ćekten af fyldmaterialet mellem bue og topplade unders├Ėgt ved hj├”lp af
skalerede modeller. P╦Üa baggrund af skaleringsteorien blev en skaleringsfaktor p╦Üa 1:12 fundet
passende. Et materialestudie blev ogs╦Üa udf├Ėrt for at ’¼ünde materialer, med materialeegenskaber

at repr├”sentere betonen i SL d├”kket og toppladen, mens gips blev valgt til at repr├”sentere den
lette beton i SL d├”kket og fyldmaterialet.
For at unders├Ėge e’¼Ćekten af fyldmaterialet blev et antal testmodeller udf├Ėrt. F├Ėrst blev en
simpel bro, kun best╦Üaende af en bue, testet som en reference. Efterf├Ėlgende blev ’¼üre fyldte
buebroer med eftersp├”ndte topplader testet, hvor h├Ėjde og stivhed af fyldmaterialet varierede.
Endelig blev en bro uden eftersp├”nding af toppladen testet. Derudover blev den teoretiske
b├”reevne for hver af de testede broer beregnet, hvor det kunne ses, at n╦Üar h├Ėjden af fyldet
mellem bue og topplade blev ├Ėget, blev b├”reevnen ogs╦Üa ├Ėget, hvorimod variationen af stivheden
ikke havde en e’¼Ćekt p╦Üa den teoretiske b├”reevne. Det kunne ogs╦Üa ses, at eftersp├”ndingen af
toppladen ogs╦Üa resulterede i en ├Ėget teoretisk b├”reevne. Endelig kunne det ud fra fors├Ėgene
ses, at en ├Ėget stivhed af fyldmaterialet resulterede i en st├Ėrre b├”reevne, hvilket en ├Ėget h├Ėjde
af fyldet ligeledes gjorde.
Under fors├Ėgene blev forskelligt m╦Üaleudstyr benyttet til at indhente data for ’¼éytning og t├Ėjning.
Computerprogrammet ARAMIS 2D og 3D m╦Üalte ’¼éytninger samt t├Ėjninger, mens LVDTŌĆÖere
blev anvendt til at m╦Üale ’¼éytninger forskellige steder i forskellige retninger. Ud fra ’¼éytningerne

mellem 1/4 punktet og 3/4 punktet uafh├”ngigt af de ├”ndrede parametre.

1
Introduction
1
Introduction
Due to cost and labour the extend of applying arch bridges have been limited for the last 50
years in Denmark. Instead beams and slabs are very common in the structural industry. These
structures are very steel consuming, produce a large amount of CO2, are limited for relative
small spans and the structural expression is very di’¼Ćerent from an arch bridge.
These di’¼āculties can be solved by introducing the super light pearl chain structures developed
by the Technical University of Denmark and Abeo A/S cf. Hertz and Halding, 2014 [1].
In this project a scaled model of a super light pearl chain arch bridge with a span to rise ratio
equal to 1:13 was studied, in order to investigate the e’¼Ćect of ’¼ülling material between the arch
consisting of 8 SL elements and a top plate. It was assumed that the ’¼ülled sprandrel arch
bridge behaved as a sandwich structure, where the ’¼ülling was able to transfer the stresses as
shear stresses. This would result in decreasing normal stresses in the arch and top plate and
hereby result in an increased theoretical bearing capacity.
In order to con’¼ürm or deny the
mentioned assumptions a number of tests of scaled models were developed. The scaled models
were constructed in order to minimize the use of material, heavy equipment and taking up to
much space in the laboratory. Due to scaling theory for strength models the bridges were only
scaled geometrically with a factor of 1:12. From the theory for strength models it was also
given that new materials similar to the materials of the prototype had to be found. After a
very time consuming process for material selection the following materials were chosen for the
models: Mortar was used as concrete in the SL decks and as top plate, while alabaster gypsum
was used as light aggregate concrete in the SL decks and as ’¼ülling material. Since the material
properties of the mortar depended on the curing time, it was important that the bridges were
cast and tested according to exactly the same schedule. In appendix B a plan is shown stating
in which order and on which day of the week the di’¼Ćerent tasks should be executed.
In order to study the e’¼Ćect of the ’¼ülling material four tests were developed. Two tests where
the height of the ’¼ülling material varied and two where the material properties for the ’¼ülling
material varied. A simple bridge only consisting of an arch was also tested as a reference, this
bridge is referred to as the Simple Bridge. The arch and the top plate in the scaled models
were post tensioned with a 3 mm steel wire to a force of 900 N. The e’¼Ćect of post tensioning
was evaluated also by studying a bridge without post tensioning of the top plate. The bridge
is in the report referred to as Bride 3B and was constructed with the exact same properties as
Bridge 3. The only di’¼Ćerence was the lack of the post tensioning wire in Bridge 3B.
A test matrix is shown in table 1.1 where the name of each bridge tested appears.
Table 1.1: Test matrix to study the e’¼Ćect of ’¼ülling material between arch and top plate.

==PAGE:14==
1
Introduction
mm ’¼ülling between arch and top plate at the crown. It is also seen that the water/gypsum ratio
was changed in order to investigate the e’¼Ćect of varying the material properties.
The project included various aspects, and a time schedule was therefore created in order to
illustrate how the time was distributed within the di’¼Ćerent tasks. The time schedule was con-
ducted as a Gantt diagram, and is shown in appendix A.
During the development of the models a pretest referred to as Bridge 0 was cast. Bridge 0 was
constructed with the same properties as Bridge 1, and was tested in order to determine the
loading rate during the tests of the bridges. The loading rate was controlled by deformations
in order to see a more detailed development of the behaviour when cracks and failure occurred.
From the test of Bridge 0 the loading rate was determined to 0.01 mm/s.
In order to test the mentioned bridges and their behaviours, they were loaded with a point load
in the 3/4 point. During the tests it was assumed that the loaded side would move downwards
while the non loaded side would move upwards. Due to this behaviour for displacements, com-
pression was assumed in the top plate and tension in the arch at the loaded side, and opposite
for the non loaded side. In order to study, if this was the case, a software called ARAMIS was
used for both the 2D and 3D system. This software was used to study displacement and strain
of the bridges. The displacement was also measured by means of Linear variable di’¼Ćerential
transformers (LVDTs).
As mentioned, it was assumed that the ’¼ülling material could transfer the stresses from the top
plate as shear stresses down through the ’¼ülling, which would result in an increasing bearing
capacity. Hereby it was also assumed, that the theoretical bearing capacity was increased when
the height of the ’¼ülling was changed from 0 mm to 20 mm. Likewise it was assumed, that
the bearing capacity was increased for increasing sti’¼Ćness of the ’¼ülling material. As mentioned
the e’¼Ćect of post tensioning the top plate was also being investigated, where it was assumed
that the theoretical bearing capacity would decrease if the top plate was not post tensioned. In
order to investigate, if these above mentioned assumptions were valid, hand calculations of the
theoretical bearing capacity were developed for each of the tested bridges. These theoretical
bearing capacities were then compared to the actual results achieved from the tests.
1.1
The Prototype Bridge Structure
The bridge structure investigated consisted of an arch developed by the pearl chain concept,
’¼ülling material and a top plate as shown in ’¼üg. 1.1
Figure 1.1: Concept of the arch bridge.
2
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:15==
1
Introduction
The scaled models were designed based on the 13 m long prototype arch, which was constructed
and tested at Perstrup Beton IndutriŌĆÖs facilities in Djursland.
The prototype arch had a rise to span ratio equal to 1:13, and consisted of super light decks
(SL decks), developed by the Technical University of Denmark and Abeo A/S. The SL deck
consists of blocks of light aggregate concrete with a density of 600 kg/m3 and a strength of
3 MPa. The blocks are made with a curved shape as shown in ’¼üg. 1.2. Pre-stressing strands
with a ultimate strength of 1860 MPa are placed between the light aggregate concrete, and
are pre tensioned to 1163 MPa. A curved post tensioned duct is also placed between the light
blocks as seen in ’¼üg. 1.2. On top of the light concrete blocks self-compacting concrete with a
compressive strength of 55 MPa is cast until the ’¼ünal thickness of the deck is reached cf. Hertz
et al., 2014 [2]. Using light weight concrete reduces the weight of the elements without reducing
the stability or rigidity, which are some of the di’¼āculties when thinner slaps are used in order
to minimize the total weight of a normal concrete element cf. Halding et al., 2015 [3]. In the
each end of the SL elements massive concrete is used for the entire thickness of the deck for at
least 200 mm, because compressive stresses need to be transferred from one SL deck to another
in a pearl chain arch.
Figure 1.2: Cross section of the super light deck element cf. Halding and Hertz, 2015 [4].
The dimensions of the SL deck can vary according to the use. The standard heights for the SL
elements are 220 mm, 270 mm or 320 mm according to the use of the elements. Because the
SL elements in this project were used for a pearl chain arch the height was set to 270 mm. The
standard widths for the SL elements are 2.4 m for use in buildings and 1.65 m for use in pearl
chain arch bridges in order to reduce the mass of each arch.
When applying the arch bridges, they would also consist of a ’¼ülling material that levels out the
arch shape and a top plate on top as seen for the bridge constructed by Abeo A/S at Vorgod
╦ÜA.
The ’¼ülling material, which is cast on top of the arch will typically consist of a light material
e.g. air-entrained concrete. The material properties for the prototype pearl chain arch and the
’¼ülling material are listed in table 1.2.
Table 1.2: Material properties for the prototype pearl chain arch and the ’¼ülling material.

1
Introduction
1.2
The Pearl-chain Concept
In the Pearl-chain concept, a number of straight SL decks are placed in the desired arch shape,
and are subsequently connected by a post tensioned wire pulled through the ducts. The ends
of the SL deck have been given a small inclination in order to obtain the arch shape when post
tensioning the elements.
As shown in ’¼üg. 1.3, the arch in the prototype consists of 6 SL deck elements and two specially
designed abutment elements in each ends connected by a post tensioned wire through curved
duct described above. The joints between the SL decks consist of mortar having a characteristic
compressive strength of at least 55 MPa, like the strong concrete in the SL decks.
Figure 1.3: Pearl chain arch consistiong of 8 SL deck elements cf. Halding et al., 2015 [3]
The shape of the pearl chain arch is circular, but since the elements are straight, the duct
through the elements are curved in a circular arch shape with a radius corresponding to the
desired arch shape. The duct is curved in order to obtain the bene’¼üts of a circular arch regarding
self weight and distributed loads, to minimizing the moment in the arch as shown in ’¼üg. 1.4.
In the joints the direction for the post tensioning cable is perpendicular to the surface. The
post-tensioning duct contains of a number of wires each with an area of 150 mm2. The duct
for the post tensioning cable is placed in the middle of the deck with a height that ensures that
no signi’¼ücant bending moment will be created due to the cable.
Figure 1.4: Curved post tesioning duct thorugt the SL deck used in pearl chain arches cf.
Halding and Hertz, 2015 [4]
4
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark

1
Introduction
1.3
Scaling of Test Model
As described earlier the prototype arch bridge investigated in this project had a span of 13 m,
and a width of 1.65 m, with a rise to span ratio of 1:13. The dimensions of the SL deck was
(H ┬Ę W ┬Ę L) = (220 ┬Ę 1650 ┬Ę 1650)mm with the dimensions of the light concrete blocks and the
concrete as shown in ’¼üg. 1.2.
To avoid the use of heavy load machines, and taking up too much space in the testing facilities
the bridge was constructed at a reduced scale. A scaling factor of 1:12 was chosen in order to
reach a length of the bridge of approximately 1 m to ’¼üt in the test machine and also to enable
that the dimensions in the complex shape of the SL deck could be constructed in the scaled
model.
In the beginning of the project the shape of the elements were designed as shown in ’¼üg. 1.2
for the entire length of each element.
After further studies of the prototype bridge it was
decided to perform the scaled SL deck more detailed and similar to the prototype elements.
And the design was therefore changed to a shape where the ends of each deck consisted of
heavy concrete through the entire thickness. Due to the size of the test machine the scaled
model arch constructed in this project only consisted of one element in the width and 8 element
in the length. The two special designed abutment elements, in the ends of the prototype bridge,
were constructed as regular SL deck in the scaled model in order to minimize the complexity
and the extra time for creating casting moulds. Likewise the mortar, in the joints between the
SL elements, was neglected in the scaled model due to the small dimensions. In order obtain
the rise to span ratio of 1:13, the SL elements were cast with an angle of 2.2ŌŚ”in each end.
The duct, through the SL elements was not curved in the model but straight due to the small
geometric. The geometry of the scaled SL elements and the arch is shown in appendix C.
The ’¼ülling material was cast or attached in one piece on top of the arch, with a top plate on
top attached to the ’¼ülling material. The design of the ’¼ülling and top plate could be constructed
with di’¼Ćerent properties. Some of the considered designs are sketched below in ’¼üg. 1.5.
Figure 1.5: Di’¼Ćerent design properties for ’¼ülling and top plate.
The sketch to the left shows the arch, with ’¼ülling, top plate and walls on each side of the ’¼ülling.
The sketch in the middle shows a similar design but with no ending wall on each side of the
’¼ülling material. The sketch to the right shows the arch with angled ending walls. There are both
advantages and disadvantages for each design. In this project the e’¼Ćect of the ’¼ülling material
was investigated, and therefore the design in the middle was chosen, in order to minimize the
impact on the ’¼ülling from other elements as for example ending walls.
It was chosen to design the top plate in such way, that it consisted of the same material as the
strong part of the SL deck and with approximate the same height as the SL deck.
According to the theory in section 3 the geometry was the only parameter to be scaled, and
new materials similar to the materials in the prototype listed in table 1.2 had to be found.

Arch Bridges
2
Arch Bridges
As mentioned in section 1, theoretical bearing capacities have been studied for the two bridge
types tested in this project. As known a simple bridge only consisting of an arch was constructed
together with a number of ’¼ülled spandrel arch bridges. In this section the theory behind the
calculations for the bearing capacities of the two mentioned bridge types subjected to a point
load in the 3/4 point are described. It is noticed that the theoretical calculations are based on
simpli’¼üed conservative assumptions in order to obtain a lower bound solution.
2.1
Single Arch
When studying the single arch, it is assumed to be a 3 hinged arch, meaning that it is a statically
determined structure where the moment at the middle span will be taken as normal forces.
Figure 2.1: 3 hinged arch with point load in the 3/4 point
For the 3 hinged arch with a point load P in the 3/4 point as shown in ’¼üg. 2.1 the moment
distribution can be determined by eq. (2.1) and eq. (2.2) for x < 3L

The moment capacity can be determined by means of the force couple method. Since the SL
decks are not cast together but simply assembled by tensioning the steel wire it is assumed that
these joints will be the most critical for the arch. In order to take this into account it is assumed
that the cross section is cracked and the tension is taken by the wire while the compression is
taken by the mortar in the compressive zone
Ac = ╬╗ ┬Ę x ┬Ę b
(2.3)
Where
b is the width of the mortar part of the SL deck.
╬╗ is a strength parameter for rectangular stress distribution cf. Jensen, 2008 [5]
When calculating the moment capacity of the arch the strength of the gypsum blocks in the SL
deck is neglected, since at the present moment no codes exist for calculations with more than
one concrete at the time. Also the compressive strength of the gypsum is small compared to
the strength of the mortar. Thus neglecting the compressive strength of the gypsum blocks was
assessed to be a reasonable simpli’¼ücation.
In ’¼üg. 2.3 the forces acting in the arch cross section are illustrated for positive bending moment
including the normal force N from the post tensioning and the self weight of the arch, which
results in the following expression for horizontal equilibrium
FT = N + FC
(2.4)
Where
FC = ╬Ę ┬Ę fcm ┬Ę Ac
The horizontal equilibrium in eq. (2.4) is also valid for negative bending moment, the only
di’¼Ćerence is that the compressive force is acting in the bottom of the cross section instead of
the top as shown in ’¼üg. 2.3.

Figure 2.3: Forces acting on arch cross section for positive bending moment
Due to limited knowledge of the material properties of the post tensioning steel wire it is not
possible to evaluate whether it will be able to reach its full strength. Conservatively it is as-
sumed that the wire requires greater strain to obtain its full strength meaning that it will not
reach the yield stress. Based on the results from testing the bridges which are further described
in section 9, it is seen that the force in the wire does not exceed 2000 N as shown in appendix I1,
hence the tensile force in the wire is assumed to be FT = 2000N.
The assumption of the tensile force in the steel wire is a rough estimate based on results from
testing of the bridges. In order to come up with a more accurate value based on theoretical
calculations it would be necessary to perform a tension test of the steel wire. But due to the
limited project period it was not possible to run a test of the wire.
By determining the distance z between the tensile force FT and the compressive force FC, the
moment capacity of the arch can be determined by
MRd = FC ┬Ę z

2
Arch Bridges
2.2
Filled Spandrel Arch Bridge
Formerly when studying ’¼ülled spandrel arch bridges the shear strength of the ’¼ülling material
have not been taken into account, meaning that only vertical load would be transferred between
the top plate and the arch.
By assuming that the ’¼ülling material can transfer shear stresses, the entire cross section of the
bridge is utilized and the bridge can be considered as a sandwich structure.
2.2.1
Normal Force
Since the plastic state is considered a postulate of the distribution of the load is admissible.
Hence in order to simplify the calculation it is assumed that the dead load of the entire bridge
is taken by the arch. The dead load is converted in to a point load Fg acting zFg from the
support as shown in ’¼üg. 2.4.
Assuming that the arch has 3 hinges, means that the dead load is taken by a normal force in
the arch and a horizontal reaction at the support Ng
Ng = Fg ┬Ę zFg
y(L/2)
(2.6)
Figure 2.4: Force from dead load Fg taken by normal force in arch
When studying the total bridge, it is a statically indeterminate structure. In order to determine
the moment distribution caused by applying a point load P in the 3/4 point, the horizontal
reaction is considered as the redundant. By postulating the force distribution and afterwards
show that the bearing capacity is su’¼ācient in all cross sections along the bridge, a lower bound
solution is found.
By studying the bridge as a simply supported beam with a vertical point load in the 3/4 point
the moment distribution will be as illustrated in ’¼üg. 2.5.

3
Theoretical Model Scaling
3
Theoretical Model Scaling
As mentioned previously the scaled models were used to investigate the e’¼Ćect of applying ’¼ülling
material in arch bridges. The use of scaled models goes back thousands of years. However these
early models were not used in order to determine deformations and strengths of a prototype,
but in the process of planning the structures.
A structural model is de’¼üned by Janney et al. (1970) cf. Harris and Sabnis, 1999 [6] as:
A structural model is any structural element or assembly of structural elements built
to a reduced scale (in comparison with full size structures) which is to be tested, and
for which laws of similitude must be employed to interpret test results.
There are both advantages and disadvantages about structural models. One advantage could
be that small models require lighter loads, while large models require heavy loading equipment.
Large models are easy to build, compared to the scaled models that can present great di’¼āculties
in fabrication and instrumentation. It is therefore very important to plan the experiment in
details because re’¼ünement of a structural model halfway through the modelling process can be
very di’¼ācult. In order to make a good model the process can typical be broken into smaller
steps, to make sure that every wanted properties are obtained.
There are a lot of di’¼Ćerent types of structural models, depending on the function of the model.
Below a number of di’¼Ćerent model types are listed cf. Harris and Sabnis, 1999 [6]
ŌĆó Elastic model
ŌĆó Indirect model
ŌĆó Direct model
ŌĆó Strength model
ŌĆó Wind e’¼Ćects model
ŌĆó Dynamic model
ŌĆó Instructional, Research, and Design Models
ŌĆó Other Model Classi’¼ücations
In this project deformations and stresses inside the ’¼ülling material were investigated. Therefore
some of the above mentioned models were considered in order to determine how to obtain as
much similarity to the prototype as possible. As illustrated from the above list only a few of
the models are relevant in this project.
Elastic Model
The ’¼ürst model that was studied was the elastic model. In this type of model, the geometry is
scaled directly from the prototype, while the material used is made by homogeneous and elastic
material that does not necessarily correspond to the prototype material. The elastic model can
only be used for studies in the elastic area of behaviour of the prototype, which means that
it can not predict postcracking behaviour for concrete or any other behaviour in the inelastic
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
15
==PAGE:28==
3
Theoretical Model Scaling
range. Since both the elastic and inelastic range were investigated in this project the theory for
elastic model could not be used.
Indirect Model
The second model that was studied was an indirect model. This type of model is a special form of
the elastic model. For indirect models the loading does not correspond to the actual load on the
prototype structure since the load e’¼Ćects are obtained by superposition. Since the loadingŌĆÖs ef-
fect on the ’¼ülling material was investigated, great similarity to the prototype bridge was desired.
Direct Model
Another type of model that was studied was a direct model. A direct model is geometrically
similar to the prototype. And the load is applied in the same manner as to the prototype. Since
the direct model obtains similarity to the prototype it could be used for this project.
Strength Model
The last type of model that was studied was a strength model. This type of model is also called
realistic model, because it is a direct model made with materials similar to the materials of the
prototype. This means that the model can be use to predict behaviour of the prototype for all
loads until failure. The strength model requires that the ratio between the properties of the
materials used for the model corresponds to the ratios between the properties for the prototype
materials. As it is described later this is the most di’¼ācult part of making a strength model.
Since the strength model is a direct model where new materials with similarity to the prototype
is used, this scaling model type was used for in this project.
3.1
Geometric scale
As seen, for all types of models the geometry is scaled with large similarity to the prototype
bridge. The size of the scaling is varying according to the type of structure being investigated,
therefore some main rules can be followed. First it is determined if the models are used for only
elastic studies or for inelastic studies. According to Syed et al., 2012 [7] it is given that for elastic
studies signi’¼ücantly small models can be prepared but for inelastic studies the strength models
would require making relatively larger models. Afterwords the type of structure according to
the scaling factor is seen in table 3.1 cf. Harris and Sabnis, 1999 [6] below
Table 3.1: Scaling factors for di’¼Ćerent classes of structures.
Type of structure
Elastic model
Strength model
Shell rofe
1:200 to 1:50
1:30 to 1:10
Highway bridge
1:25
1:20 to 1:4
Reactor vessel
1:100 to 1:50
1:20 to 1:4
Beam/slab structures
1:25
1:10 to 1:4
Dams
1:400
1:75
Wind e’¼Ćects
1:300 to 1:50
Not applicable
As mentioned the strength model was chosen for this project and the studied bridges were
assumed to be highway bridges, a scaling factor of 1:12 was therefore chosen for the geometric
scaling.

below. According to Harris and Sabnis, 1999 [6] the load-reduction factor is the most dramatic
part since the concentrated load on a prototype is reduced in proportion to the square of the

equal to 0.25kN load on a 1:20 scaled model, which could also be determined from dimensional

Materials
4
Materials
As mentioned previously in the report it was desired to ’¼ünd materials for the scaled models
where the ratio between the material properties corresponded to the ratio of the material prop-
erties of the prototype bridge. By means of compression tests and E-modulus tests several
di’¼Ćerent materials were studied.
It was understood, that ’¼ünding materials where both sti’¼Ćness and strength corresponded to the
prototype properties, was a di’¼ācult and very time-consuming task, and it was therefore decided
to prioritize the sti’¼Ćness rather than the strength. Thus, alabaster gypsum was chosen to repre-
sent the light concrete blocks in the SL deck and the ’¼ülling material between the arch and the top
plate, while mortar was chosen to represent the heavy concrete in the SL deck and the top plate.
As listed previously the material properties for the prototype bridge were as given in table 4.1.

The main reason for choosing alabaster gypsum, as the ’¼ülling material, was the fact that the
material properties could easily be varied with the w/g ratio. And thereby the e’¼Ćect of di’¼Ćerent
material properties could be investigated. Furthermore the e’¼Ćect of varying the height of the
’¼ülling material was also investigated, which would also be relatively simple when using alabaster
gypsum.
For the light aggregate concrete in the SL deck an alabaster gypsum with w/g of 0.40 was used.
For the concrete in the SL deck mortar was used because it could easily be spread out in the
shape of the SL deck. And in order to reach similar material properties in the top plate, mortar
was also used. The adherence between the applied materials is described in section 4.10.
4.1
Mortar
The chosen mortar was a VB 10 Rapid Set Grout from PAGEL. A data description of the
product is seen in appendix E. In order to determine whether or not mortar could be used
in combination with for instance gypsum, the material properties were determined. The com-
pressive strength and ’¼éexural strength of mortar were determined according to the procedure
described in DS/EN 196-1, while for the E-modulus the procedure in DS/EN 13412 was followed.
In order to determine the material properties, 9 prisms were cast. Because of the relatively
short time period for the project, the mortar was tested after 6 days of hardening where the
mortar had not yet reached its full strength. According to the standard mortar is assumed to
be fully hardened after 28 days.
The test setup for the ’¼éexural strength test and compression test is shown in ’¼üg. 4.1. For the
’¼éexural test the distance between the supports was 100 mm. After bending the specimens into
two pieces the compression test was conducted.

(b) Compression test

4.4
Maxi Bond
Another material, that was tried, was Maxi Bond. Maxi Bond is an extremely fast and strong
glue, and it was therefore considered as a part of the SL deck. In order to determined the
material properties of the glue, one cylinder was produced. During the process it was seen,
that the glue was thick and very di’¼ācult to spread out evenly, which would be a problem when
the complex shape of the SL deck had to be constructed. In addition the glue cylinder with a
diameter of 50 mm and a height of 100 mm took more than one week to dry. The combination,
of the long drying time and the fact that the glue was very di’¼ācult to work with, led to the
conclusion that the Maxi Bond glue as a part of the SL deck was not an optimal material.
4.5

Another material, that was tested, was expanding foam. The foam is normally used as insulating
in constructions. Because the expanding foam was sprayed out, it could be shaped as desired.
In order to test the material properties for expanding foam, 3 cylinders were produced as shown
in ’¼üg. 4.8. The moulds were covered with plastic (polyethylene) to prevent the expanding foam
from attaching to the mould.

4.10
Adherence of the Materials
As described previously in the report the two materials chosen for the model bridge were mortar
and gypsum. In order to produce the arch bridge it was essential that the two materials were
attached, which was relevant three places in the bridge:
ŌĆó Between the gypsum and mortar in the SL deck
ŌĆó Between the mortar in the SL deck and the gypsum ’¼ülling
ŌĆó Between the gypsum ’¼ülling and mortar top plate
To test the adherence of gypsum on mortar a block of mortar was cast, and after one day the
gypsum was cast on top of it as shown in ’¼üg. 4.17. Due to the very compact and smooth surface
of the mortar, the gypsum could not penetrate it. Since there was no attachment between the
two materials, the gypsum could easily be lifted o’¼Ćthe mortar block.
Figure 4.17: Gypsum cast on top of mortar.
To optimize the adherence between the two materials several methods were considered and
tested. One way to optimize the adherence would be to use a retarder to make the surface
of the mortar more rough or simply roughen up the surface by hand. The latter method was
tested as shown in ’¼üg. 4.18 and was proven to be a successful way to optimize the adherence.
But this method would not be suitable in the case where a small scaled bridge was considered,
since even the smallest changes in the geometry could have great in’¼éuence.
Figure 4.18: Mortar roughened up to optimize the adherence with gypsum.
Another method that could be used to improve the adherence was to sprinkle sand on the newly
cast mortar or gypsum surface and in that way create a more rough surface. This method was
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
31
==PAGE:44==
4
Materials
not suitable for the adherence between the mortar and gypsum in the SL deck due to the design
of the mould. As it is further described later in the report, the mortar part of the SL deck was
cast in a salt dough mould and afterwards the gypsum part was cast on top of the mortar. If
sand was to be used to improve the adherence it should be sprinkled over the salt dough before
the mortar was poured in. However, sand did not stick to the salt dough and it would therefore
not be evenly distributed in the mould, but it would pile up in the bottom of the mould.
Therefore an alternative method was applied, where stirrups or in this case staples were used
to connect the two materials in the SL deck. This method was easily applied by placing the
staples in the salt dough as further described in section 5.
For the other two cases (between the arch and the ’¼ülling material and between the ’¼ülling and
the top plate) the method with the sand was at ’¼ürst considered as a suitable solution. After
casting the SL decks sand was sprinkled on top of the wet mortar and again after casting the
’¼ülling material sand was sprinkled on the wet gypsum. But, when the gypsum and mortar cured
the two materials did not behave in the same way. According to appendix E, the mortar from
PAGEL does not shrink during curing, while the gypsum however expands when it cures.
The di’¼Ćerence in the material behaviour resulted in a shear force between the top plate and
’¼ülling material. After removing Bridge 0 from the mould, it was seen that the sand did not
apply su’¼ācient shear resistance to take the force, which resulted in the two materials separating
as seen in ’¼üg. 4.19.
Figure 4.19: The top plate and ’¼ülling material separated due to di’¼Ćerences in material behaviour
During the test of Bridge 0, where the bridge was loaded in the quarter point, it was seen that
when the bridge started de’¼éecting and tension occurred between the top plate and the ’¼ülling
at the non-loaded side, the top plate was lifted from the ’¼ülling material as seen in ’¼üg. 4.20.
32
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:45==
4
Materials
Figure 4.20: Top plate separated from ’¼ülling during test
It was necessary to improve the adherence between the top plate and the ’¼ülling material, which
was carried out by applying paper clips as described in section 5.2.
After paper clips were placed to connect the top plate to the ’¼ülling material, it was seen that
during the test of Bridge 1 the ’¼ülling material separated from the arch as shown in ’¼üg. 4.21. In

5
Development of Moulds
5
Development of Moulds
5.1
SL deck
Due to the complex cross section of the SL deck, which is further described in section 1, the
production of the moulds is challenging. The decks have a very curved cross section and with
a scaling factor of 1:12 the details of the deck will be small which will be hard to produce with
su’¼ācient accuracy if the mould is cut out of wood. 3D printing is therefore an obvious method
to produce the moulds. It can print in any given shape with an accuracy of up to 0.02 mm.
5.1.1
Design 1
The idea with the ’¼ürst mould design was to 3D print mould parts of PLA plastic which could
be assembled into a mould as illustrated in ’¼üg. 5.1. The mortar could then be poured into the
mould and when it was cured the mould could simply be taken apart and the gypsum could
afterwards be cast on top of the mortar.
However, as mentioned previously in the report, 3D printing was a very time consuming process,
meaning that the production of one mould would take approximately 30 hours. The bridge
consisted of 8 SL deck parts, which should be cast at the same time from the same batch to
achieve identical strength and sti’¼Ćness at the time of testing. Therefore a minimum of 8 moulds
were needed for the casting of the bridge. Since the 3D printers were often occupied by teaching,
the access was limited to two days a week, which meant that it would take at least 4 weeks
to create 8 moulds. Due to the relatively short project period this casting method was not
optimal.
Figure 5.1: The ’¼ürst design of the PLA plastic moulds.
5.1.2
Design 2
In the second design focus was on creating a mould that was fast to produce but still had the
accuracy that could be achieved with 3D printing. Therefore it was chosen to 3D print a piston
of PLA plastic as shown in ’¼üg. 5.2.
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
35
==PAGE:48==
5
Development of Moulds
Figure 5.2: The second design of the moulds.
The piston was pushed down in a salt dough which was mixed according to the recipe shown in
appendix D. The salt dough was placed in a squared mould with two inclined ends, each with
an angle of 2.2ŌŚ”. When the piston was pushed down in the salt dough, the salt dough obtained
the shape of the SL deck. In order to get the gypsum to cling to mortar later in the casting
process small stirrups were placed in the salt dough, which is further described in section 4.10.
Also a knitting needle was installed in the mould which was to be pulled out after the casting
and would hereby create the post tensioning duct through the element. This is shown in ’¼üg. 5.3.
Figure 5.3: Placing of stirrups and duct in the salt dough.
Afterwards the mortar was poured into the salt dough mould. And after one day the salt dough
was removed and gypsum was poured over the mortar. In this way the 8 moulds was produced
fast and cheap.
The ’¼ürst test of this casting method showed that using a knitting needle to create the duct was
not a suitable solution. After the mortar had cured the knitting needle was stuck inside the
deck.
To avoid this from happening again the knitting needle was replaced by a hollow pipe, which
could be left in the mortar after curing. Since the pipe would still be in the deck during testing
it had to be a weak material that would not a’¼Ćect the strength and sti’¼Ćness of the deck. It was
therefore decided to use plastic straws with a diameter of 5 mm.
36
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:49==
5
Development of Moulds
Up until this point the design of the SL deck was kept as simple as possible with the gypsum
continuing through the entire element. But in order to improve the transfer of the compressive
stresses from one element to the next, the design was changed so that the element had mortar
for the full height at the outer 16.7 mm of each end as shown in ’¼üg. 5.4.
Figure 5.4: Final design of mortar deck.
In ’¼üg. 5.4 the ’¼ünal design of the mortar part of the SL deck is shown. The stirrups was replaced
by staples simply because they were easier and faster to place in the salt dough.
Afterwards the gypsum was cast into the grooves of the mortar decks. The ’¼ünal SL deck with
both mortar and gypsum is shown in ’¼üg. 5.5.
Figure 5.5: Final design of SL deck.
During testing it was found that additional adherence was needed between the arch and ’¼ülling
material, which is further described later in the report. Therefore paper clips were placed in

not in level with the sides of the mould as shown in ’¼üg. 5.8.

6
Assembling the Bridge
6
Assembling the Bridge
After the SL decks were cast using the method described in section 5 they were assembled to
an arch with a 3 mm steel wire. The steel wire was post tensioned by means of a tensioning
device, which consisted of a nut and a bolt with a drilled hole through it. In each end the wire
was fastened with a washer and two wire locks. In this way the steel wire was tightened when
the bolt was screwed out of the nut. In order to monitor the tension applied to the wire a ring
with resistance gauges was installed between the bridge and the tensioning device as shown in
’¼üg. 6.1.
Figure 6.1: The tensioning device and the ring with resistance gauges applied to post tension
the bridge.
When the wire was tensioned a normal compressive force was applied to the arch at each end.
As seen in ’¼üg. 6.1 it was the washer with a diameter of 30 mm that was transferring the tension
from the wire to the arch as a compressive force meaning that it was applied as a concentrated
load and not distributed over the entire cross section of the SL deck. In order to distribute
the load more evenly across the cross section a steel plate having the dimensions of the cross
section of the SL deck could have been applied at the ends of the arch. Using this method the
compressive force would be applied to the entire cross section of the SL deck, given that the
steel plate had su’¼ācient thickness and thereby su’¼ācient sti’¼Ćness to prevent it from de’¼éecting
at the ends when the wire was tensioned.
Due to limited knowledge of the above mentioned issue in the design face only the washer seen
in ’¼üg. 6.1 was applied to the arch. At the point where the issue was brought to attention, the
test setup consisting of a steel beam with steel blocks had already been produced, and it would
not be possible to ’¼üt in the steel plates.
Also the top plate was post tensioned using a 3 mm steel wire and the tensioning device shown
in ’¼üg. 6.1, which resulted in a compressive force in the top plate. As described in section 4.10
paper clips were placed in the ’¼ülling during casting in order to optimize the adherence between
the ’¼ülling material and the top plate. These paper clips transferred the normal force, applied
by post tensioning, from the top plate to the ’¼ülling material, meaning that the upper part of
the ’¼ülling material was also a’¼Ćected by the post tensioning.
It was assumed that the post tensioning would have an e’¼Ćect on the bearing capacity of the
bridge, and the load applied with the tensioning device both before and during the test was
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
43
==PAGE:56==
6
Assembling the Bridge
therefore monitored. As mentioned previously the load was monitored with a ring with resis-
tance gauges. By means of Wheatstones bridge the resistance gauge measured the load applied
to the ring. The resistance was measured in volt which was converted into a force by calibrating
the ring with weights. It was necessary to calibrate to a force larger than the one applied with
the post tensioning, to ensure that the correlation was valid for the entire interval.
Based on the post tensioning of the 13 meter prototype bridge an estimate was conducted,
stating that the 1:12 model bridge should be post tensioned to approximately 900 N. This post
tensioning load was applied to both the arch and the top plate. Since the post tensioning was
applied by hand it was di’¼ācult to reach exactly 900 N. In section 9 the exact post tensioning
loads applied to the individual arches and top plates are further described.
To ensure that the post tensioning force did not exceed the calibration load, the calibration
was carried out by loading and unloading from 0 to 2300 N in steps of 200 N three times which
resulted in a correlation between the signal output of the ring and the applied force.
In ’¼üg. 6.2 the three loading-unloading cycles are shown. It is seen that the graph for the ’¼ürst
loading stood out from the other two loadings. Since it was the ’¼ürst time the ring was loaded
it might have de’¼éected slightly during the loading process causing the signal to change. It is
seen that for the second and third loading the ring had adjusted to the load and resulted in a
very clear correlation between the load applied and the signal.
Figure 6.2: Correlation between signal and force applied to ring number 1.
As mentioned the tensioning device and the ring with the resistance gauges were applied on
both the arch and the top plate. At the arch the device was mounted when assembling the
SL decks while at the top plate the device is not mounted until the bridge was removed from
the mould right before testing. Since two bridges were cast and tested at the same day it was
necessary to have 3 rings. The calibration was therefore repeated with two additional rings.
The loading-unloading graphs for these rings are shown in appendix G and the correlations

7
Testing
As mentioned earlier ’¼üve bridges were tested in order to study the e’¼Ćect of the ’¼ülling material

material or top plate, this test is referred to as the Simple Bridge. Then two tests with varied
height of the ’¼ülling, and two tests with varied material properties for the ’¼ülling material were
developed. The test matrix is shown in table 1.1, where the name of each test is mentioned.
Additionally a bridge identical to Bridge 3 consisting of same materials and same geometry but
without the post tensioning force in top plate was also tested and referred to as Bridge 3B.

7.1
Test Setup
During testing of the bridges it was important that the bridges could not move in horizontal
direction at the supports. To avoid any horizontal movement the bridge was placed on a steel
beam with steel blocks welded on. When the bridge was loaded by a point load the horizontal
force at the supports was taken as a tensile force in the steel beam. The supporting steel beam
is shown in ’¼üg. 7.1, where the test bridge is placed between the steel blocks. As seen in ’¼üg. 7.1
the steel blocks were covered with abrasive paper to avoid the bridge from moving vertically in
the supports during loading. The steel blocks were made with an extra height, in order to ’¼üt
all the 5 bridges, if the length of the bridges varied slightly. Another solution for the varying
length of the bridges could be to make one or both of the blocks able to move. But since all of
the SL decks were cast in the same moulds the length of the bridges did almost not vary, and
the steel blocks were therefore welded on the beam, and the risk of a horizontal movement was
therefore neglected.
(a) Steel blocks
(b) Supporting beam
Figure 7.1: The supporting beam to avoid horizontal movement during the tests.
The machine used for the test was a four column universal test machine which is shown in
’¼üg. 7.2.
As already mentioned the bridges were loaded in the quarter point.
The rate of
the tests were controlled by displacement, in order to see a more detailed development of the
behaviour when cracks and failure occurred. Based on the pretest of Bridge 0, the loading rate
was set to 0.01mm/s.
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
47
==PAGE:60==
7
Testing
Figure 7.2: Test machine
7.2
Measuring
In order to obtain as many data from the tests as possible, di’¼Ćerent measuring equipment were
used. The software ARAMIS was used to record the strains and displacements of the bridges.
Both the 2D and the 3D version of the software were applied. As mentioned the test machine
was a four column machine with a distance between the columns of 0.5 m, and since the bridges
had a length of around 1 m the middle of the arch and the support at the loaded side were
hidden behind the columns. Additional measurements of the displacements were measured with
Linear Variable Di’¼Ćerential Transformers (LVDTs). The placing of the LVDTs varied for the
Simple Bridge and the other bridges, which is further described in section 9.
7.2.1
ARAMIS
As mentioned the software ARAMIS both 2D and 3D were used. ARAMIS is an equipment
consisting of either one camera for 2D and two cameras for 3D, that takes pictures during the
test. Afterwards the pictures are analysed in the software ARAMIS, where a lot of di’¼Ćerent
results can be found.
The cameras were placed on tripods in order to prevent movements during the tests, since it
was very important that the cameras were in exact same position on each picture. The setup
for the 3D cameras is described in B. Pease, 2007 [12] and is shown in ’¼üg. 7.3.
Figure 7.3: Setup for ARAMIS 3D according to the guide.
In this project both 3D and 2D ARAMIS were used on each half side of the bridge. Due to the
columns on the test machine the cameras could only take pictures on one half of the bridge,
and therefore 3D ARAMIS was used on the loaded side of the bridge while 2D ARAMIS was
48
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:61==
7
Testing
used on the non loaded side. Before the 3D ARAMIS could be used it was very important that
the cameras were calibrated. For the calibration a special calibration cross was used. The cross
consisted of a black cross with white marks on. The cross was rotated and twisted according to
the guide in the software. Even though the cameras were calibrated there would still be some
uncertainties on the results. In order to monitor that, 5 pictures were taking before loading
each bridge, in order to see the small uncertainties, and also to make sure that the software was
able to read the surface of the bridge.
As mentioned previously the loading rate was found from the pretest as 0.01 mm/s, in order
for the tests to last for about 4 minutes. According to that the interval of pictures taken in
ARAMIS 3D was set to one picture per 5 sec., and for the 2D ARAMIS a picture was taken for
every 50 N-100 N increase of the load.
To make sure that the surface of the bridge was computable with the ARAMIS system a pattern
was painted on the surface. At the pretest of Bridge 0 a pattern was made by spray painting
on the surface as shown in ’¼üg. 7.4.
Figure 7.4: Spray painted pattern on bridge.
From the pretest it was found that ARAMIS could not read the surface, when the pattern was
made by spray paint, and therefore some new methods were tested. Both dots and squares were
painted with a permanent marker on small samples and tested with ARAMIS. It was found
that neither of the methods provided acceptable results. Therefore a steel plate with small
holes was used as a template and spray painting was used. The pattern with painted dots is
seen in ’¼üg. 7.5.
Figure 7.5: Patter applied by steel plate with holes and spray painting.
By applying this pattern the ARAMIS software were able to read the entire surface, thus this
method was used for all the bridges.

7.2.2
LVDTs
The displacements of the bridges were also measured with Linear Variable Di’¼Ćerential Trans-

can be determined. The LVDTs are attached to an arm that can be placed on the test machine
due to magnetism, in order to be able to place the LVDTs in any given position.
For each bridge 5 LVDTs were used to measure displacements in di’¼Ćerent directions.
The
placing of the LVDTs on the bridges di’¼Ćered from the Simple Bridge to the bridges 1 to 4. The
placing for each tested bridge is shown in section 9 along with the results. In ’¼üg. 7.6 a picture
of LVDTs measuring the vertical displacement of the non loaded side is shown.

Theoretical Bearing Capacity
8
Theoretical Bearing Capacity
Based on the theory covered in section 2 a theoretical bearing capacity of each of the bridges
tested in this project was determined.
The theoretical capacity is a lower bound solution,
meaning that it would be expected that during the real tests, the bridges would have greater
capacities than the theoretically determined ones.
8.1
Single Arch
The positive and negative moment capacity of the single arch were determined using the theory
described in section 2.1. The entire calculation is shown in appendix H.1. As shown in ’¼üg. 8.1
the positive moment capacity was reached by applying a point load of Pmax = 109N.
Figure 8.1: Moment distribution and moment capacities of single arch
In table 8.1 the positive and negative moment capacities are shown along with the maximum
moments that occurred in the arch by applying the maximum load Pmax.

9.2
Strains
As mentioned previously the strains in the bridges were monitored using ARAMIS 3D around
the loaded side and ARAMIS 2D at the non-loaded side.
In general there are great uncertainties about how to interpret the results of the major and
58
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:71==
9
Test Results
minor strains obtained with the ARAMIS software. Some people believe that it is not actual
strains but rather displacement vectors the major and minor strain applications indicate.
It was therefore very di’¼ācult to use the stain color plots from ARAMIS to analyse the magnitude
and direction of the stresses in the structure, but they could be applied to illustrate where the
larger strains occurred and to show where a crack occurred before it was possible to actual see
a physical crack.
In order to evaluated the stress distribution in the ’¼ülling material of the bridges, the strains
were computed manually by de’¼üning points in ARAMIS in a half star shape as seen in ’¼üg. 9.7
and measuring the mutual distance between the points. The strains within the measured area
could then be calculated by the general expression for strain
ŽĄ = L0 ŌłÆL
L0
(9.1)
Figure 9.7: Points manually de’¼üned in ARAMIS to calculate strains.
When evaluating the strains in the ’¼ülling material, it was desired to study the plastic state
before any potential cracks developed. It was therefore decided to calculate the strains when
the bridge was loaded with 20% of its maximum load.
The strains were measured from 4 to 6 di’¼Ćerent base points in the ’¼ülling material as illustrated
in ’¼üg. 9.8. For some of the bridges the measurements from point C1B and C5B were omitted
due to the limited height of the ’¼ülling material.
Figure 9.8: Base points for strain measurements in ’¼ülling material.
In ’¼üg. 9.9 the strain measurements around base point C4 for Bridge 2 are shown. The strains are
illustrated as vectors with the right mutual direction and magnitude. The red color indicates
tension while the black color indicates compression. In appendix appendix J.2 the strains in
the ’¼ülling material are shown for all bridges.
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
59
==PAGE:72==
9
Test Results
Figure 9.9: Strains measured around base point C4 in Bridge 2
Besides the strain measurements in the ’¼ülling material, the normal strains in the arch and the
top plate were also calculated by means of the procedure described above, where points were
de’¼üned manually. The strain measurements in the arch and the top plate enabled evaluation of
where tensile and compressive stresses occurred, and if it corresponded to what was expected
based on the theory.
In the arch the measured lengths were divided such that the strains in the SL decks and
the strains in the joints were studied individually. The top plate was separated into lengths
corresponding to the length of the SL deck.
When studying the simple bridge it was expected that arch would be exposed to tension in the
bottom of the element and compression in the top on the loaded side and opposite on the non-
loaded side. In order to evaluate if the expectations were met the strains were measured both
in the top and the bottom of the elements. But with the default facet settings in ARAMIS 3D,
only a few number of facets were provided in the height of the arch, therefore when studying the
simple bridge the facet size was set to 12 and the facet step to 8 for the 3D version in both X-
and Y-direction, which provided a smaller mesh. For ARAMIS 2D the default facet settings 7
facets were provided over the height of the arch, which was considered to be su’¼ācient, hence for
the 2D version the default settings were retained. A consequence of changing the facet settings
was that there was more area, where ARAMIS could not measure the surface. Therefore the
outer SL deck on the loaded side of the arch was not measured.
In ’¼üg. 9.10 the strains in the simple bridge are illustrated. It is seen that the results of the
normal strains in the arch partly met the expectations. On the loaded side the upper part of
the arch was subjected to compression as expected, while the bottom part was subjected to
a combination of tension and compression. Also it is seen that tension occurred in the joints,
which corresponded to the assumptions mentioned in the theoretical calculation and to the color
plot in appendix K.1.
Figure 9.10: Normal strains in Simple Bridge when loaded with 20% of max. load.
60
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:73==
9
Test Results
For the ’¼ülled spandrel arch bridges the strains were measured in the top plate and the arch
using the same method as for the Simple Bridge. The calculations of the normal strains in all
bridges and the illustrations are shown in appendix J.1.
As previously mentioned 5 pictures where taken with the ARAMIS cameras of each bridge
before loading it. In order to determine the inaccuracy the strains within these 5 pictures were
calculated for both the 2D- and the 3D pictures. The results of the inaccuracies are shown in
appendix J.6, where it was seen that for the 2D software strains up to 0.3% occurred in the
non-loaded situation, while for the 3D software strains up to 0.04% occurred, meaning that
when studying the behaviour of the bridge during loading, one should be critical of strains of
these magnitudes and smaller.

The reason why some of the bridges were tensioned with a force larger than the intended 900

9.5.1
Test observations for the Simple Bridge
From the pretest it was found that the loading rate controlled by displacement should be 0.01
mm/s. After testing for a couple of minutes it was seen that this rate was too slow for the
simple bridge, the loading rate was therefore increased to 0.1 mm/s after about 13 minutes of
testing.
A photo at the end of the test showing the total displacement of the simple bridge is shown in
’¼üg. 9.15. Pictures from the test and the development of the load on the bridge and the force in
the tension device as a function of time was plotted and shown in appendix I.1.
Figure 9.15: Displacements and cracks on the Simple Bridge at the end of the test.

loaded side.

10
Discussion
10
Discussion
As illustrated in the test matrix shown in table 1.1 only one test of each bridge type was tested,
and it was therefore not possible to make conclusions directly from the tests. Instead the ex-
pected behaviour due to the theoretical calculations of the bearing capacity was discussed and
compared in relation to the result achieved by the tests. Likewise the behaviours obtained for
the bridges could not be directly transferred to the prototype bridges, due to a number of devi-
ations between casting and assembling of the scaled models compared to the prototype bridge.
But the results from the scaled tests could be used as an indication of what might be expected as
a possible behaviour for full size bridge. In order to con’¼ürm or deny if the behaviours observed
for the scaled models could be expected on the full size bridges further investigations would
have to be carried out as mentioned later in section 12.
10.1
Displacements of the bridges during loading
As mentioned previously the displacements of the bridges at 5 di’¼Ćerent locations on the bridge
was measured with both LVDTs and the ARAMIS software 2D and 3D. As shown in section 9
the movement in all three directions (x,y,z) were measured. Since the bridges are loaded in
the 3/4 point it was assumed that the bridge would be exposed to at downwards negative
displacement along the y-axis on the loaded side and opposite for the non loaded side where a
positive upward displacement was assumed. A measurement had also been developed along the
x-axis on the loaded side. Here it was assumed that a negative displacement would occur due to
the downwards displacement in the y-direction. At last the displacements along the z-axis was
measured where was assumed that no movement would occur because the bridge was loaded
equally over the entire width of the bridge. In appendix I the results from the displacements
measured with the LVDTs are illustrated together with the displacements from ARAMIS. In
general it is seen that the assumed behaviour at each location was con’¼ürmed. Likewise it is
also seen that the two measuring methods were similar for most of the bridges at each location.
As mentioned earlier 5 pictures were taking in ARAMIS before loading the bridges in order to
determine the uncertainty of the measurements. In table 10.1 the maximum uncertainties for
each location on respectively the Simple Bridge and the Bridges from 1 to 4 are listed.
Table 10.1: Maximum uncertainty of the measurements from the ARAMIS software 2D and
3D.
Software
Simple Bridge
Bridge 1 to 4

┬▒0.013

┬▒0.199

The maximum uncertainties shown in table 10.1 indicated that the software version 3D was
more accurate that the 2D version for the Bridges 1 to 4. Since the 2D software could only
measure the (x,y)-direction, it was very important that the measured surface was perpendicular
to the camera. For the Simple Bridge it was seen that the accuracy of the measurements were
almost equal for both software versions. Since the Simple Bridge only consisted of an arch the
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark

Discussion
measurements were taking directly in front of the camera. While for the ’¼ülled spandrel arches
the measured surface was larger, meaning that the outer areas were not measured perpendicular.
Comparing the uncertainties with the test results plotted in appendix I it was seen that the
displacements, which occurred during the tests were much larger than the inaccuracy for all
locations and they were therefore neglected.
As mentioned previously the LVDT 1 was located behind one of the columns of the test machine
and it was therefore not possible to perform measurements from either ARAMIS software 2D
or 3D at the exact location. Therefore two point on each side of the mid span were developed.
An example of a measurement corresponding to location 1 is shown for Bridge 3B below.

points. This was a tendency for most of the bridges at location 1 as illustrated in the plots
in appendix I. It was therefore di’¼ācult to use the ARAMIS measurements at that location in
order to determine the displacement at the mid span.

Figure 10.2: Displacement from ARAMIS and LVDT at location 3 on Bridge 4.
