Discussion
In ’¼üg. 10.2 it is seen that the displacement from the LVDT was ’¼ürst decreasing and then after
200 second increasing again, where the ARAMIS measuring for displacement was decreasing
during the entire testing period. During the test the bridges moved a bit when large cracks
occurred and that could result in slightly movements of the LVDT, meaning that the LVDT at
location was moved and hereby did not measure horizontally.
10.2
In’¼éuence of varying the height of the ’¼ülling
As mentioned previously in table 1.1 the e’¼Ćect of varying the height of the ’¼ülling material was
investigated. Two bridges with a ’¼ülling height of 0 mm was tested, and compared to the two
bridges with 20 mm extra height of ’¼ülling. By studying the theoretically calculated bearing
capacities in table 8.2, the expected e’¼Ćect of varying the height of the ’¼ülling material was stud-
ied. By comparing Bridge 1 to Bridge 2 and Bridge 3 and 3B to Bridge 4 it was seen, that
when increasing the ’¼ülling height it was expected that the load bearing capacity of the bridge
was also increased. This was due to the increased internal moment arm between the force couple.
According to the test results the expected behaviour was reached. For the bridges with the
strong ’¼ülling material the increase of the height caused a greater bearing capacity as expected.
From test results shown in section 9 it was seen that for Bridge 3 the maximum load was about
3000 N, and for Bridge 3B the maximum load was about 4200 N, while for Bridge 4, where
the ’¼ülling height was increased with 20 mm the maximum load was just under 9000 N. For
the bridges with the weaker mix of gypsum ’¼ülling, the increased height also caused a greater
bearing capacity as expected. For Bridge 1 the maximum load applied was about 3500 N during
test 1A and about 2900 N during test 1B, and for Bridge 2 the maximum load was about 6000 N.
10.3
In’¼éuence of varying the water/gypsum ratio
Not only the height of the ’¼ülling was studied. But as mentioned earlier the e’¼Ćect of varying the
material properties was also investigated as shown in table 1.1. According to the theoretically
calculated bearing capacity shown in table 8.2 the e’¼Ćect of varying the strength and sti’¼Ćness
of the ’¼ülling material was investigated. Here it was seen that Bridge 1 with w/g = 0.3 and
Bridge 3 with w/g = 0.25, both with the low height of the ’¼ülling, had the same theoretical
bearing capacity. An likewise for Bridge 2 and Bridge 4 with respectively the weak and strong
gypsum and the 20 mm extra height of ’¼ülling. As known form the calculations in appendix H
the most critical part on the bridges were in either the arch or top plate and not in the ’¼ülling.
The change in the material properties of the ’¼ülling material was therefore not expected to have
a signi’¼ücant e’¼Ćect on the theoretical bearing capacity.
From the test results in section 9 the behaviour of each bridge is described and the maximum
load applied is stated. For the bridges with 20 mm extra height between arch and top plate the
change in w/g ratio from 0.3 to 0.25 caused a greater bearing capacity. From the test results
shown in section 9 it is seen that for Bridge 2 with w/g = 0.30 the maximum load was about
6000 N, while for Bridge 4, with a w/g = 0.25 the maximum load was just under 9000 N. For
the bridges with connection between arch and top plate, the change in w/g ratio from 0.3 to
0.25 did not cause a greater bearing capacity. For Bridge 1 with w/g = 0.3 the maximum load
applied was about 3500 N during test 1A and about 2900 N during test 1B, while for Bridge 3
with w/g = 0.25 the maximum load was about 3000 N, meaning that the load bearing capacity
was reduced when increasing the strength and sti’¼Ćness of the ’¼ülling material. As mentioned
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
79
==PAGE:92==
10
Discussion
previously Bridge 3 was asymmetric, meaning that the height of the ’¼ülling material was reduced
at some places and increased at others. This could have had an e’¼Ćect on the maximum load,
and it was therefore di’¼ācult to see if the varied material properties for the ’¼ülling material had
a positive e’¼Ćect on the bearing capacity.
By studying the calculations for Bridge 3 in appendix H.4 it can be seen that an o’¼Ćset to the
left of the mid span would result in larger positive and negative peak values of the moment. As
mentioned earlier the moment was taken by a force couple NP in the arch and the top plate.
Meaning that increasing values of the moment resulted in increasing positive and negative values
of the normal force couple, and the bearing capacity was therefore decreased.
According to the achieved knowledge, the e’¼Ćect of decreasing the w/g ratio observed for Bridge
2 and 4 would also be expected for Bridge 1 and Bridge 3.
10.4
In’¼éuence of tensioning the top plate
As mentioned earlier a bridge with no tension in the top plate named Bridge 3B was also tested
in order to discuss the e’¼Ćect. Bridge 3B was constructed with the same properties as Bridge 3,
meaning that the ’¼ülling material had w/g = 0.25 and 0 mm height of the ’¼ülling. From the test
results in section 9 the maximum load applied on Bridge 3 was about 3000 N, while for Bridge
3B the maximum load applied was about 4200 N.
In order to be able to compare these results a the theoretical bearing capacity was calculated for
Bridge 3B according to the procedure described in section 2 but where no normal force Npost =
900N was applied in the top plate. The calculations for Bridge 3B is shown in appendix H.6
and the maximum load Pmax and the corresponding postulated value of the horizontal reaction
RH is listed in table 10.2 together with the results for Bridge 3 presented in section 8 .
Table 10.2: Theoretical bearing capacities of ’¼ülled spandrel arch bridges for Bridge 3B and
Bridge 3.
Bridge 3B
Bridge 3
Pmax [N]
680
850
RH [N]
1650
2200
By comparing the results for the theoretical bearing capacity in table 10.2 to the test results
of maximum applied load on the bridges, it is seen that these results were con’¼éicting. From
the theoretical calculation it was expected that the bearing capacity would decrease for Bridge
3B compared to Bridge 3. But looking at the test results the direct opposite behaviour was
observed, where the bearing capacity was increased for Bridge 3B compared to Bridge 3. An
explanation for the unexpected behaviour during the tests could be due to the asymmetry of
Bridge 3, which was mentioned previously in the report. It was therefore di’¼ācult to con’¼ürm or
deny the expected behaviour of tensioning the top plate according to the theoretical calculation
by the test results.
10.5
Failure of bridges
The locations and time of where and when cracks and failure occurred were studied in order to
determine if the ’¼ülling height or material properties had an impact. The locations of the cracks
and an indication of at what time each crack occurred are illustrated for each of the bridges in
80
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:93==
10
Discussion
section 9. At ’¼ürst sight it was noticed that failure of Bridge 3 shown in ’¼üg. 9.23 di’¼Ćered from
the rest of the bridges. As mentioned before the arch was asymmetric in Bridge 3 which also
resulted in an asymmetric failure. On the non loaded side of Bridge 3 the cracks occurred in
the 3/8 point while they on the loaded side occurred at the 3/4 point.
Form the theoretical calculations of the bearing capacity it was seen that the critical point for
positive moment was at the 3/4 point, while it for the negative moment was to the right of
the 3/8 point. The location of the most critical point for negative moment varied with the
postulated value of the horizontal reaction RH. For increasing values of the horizontal reaction
the critical point for negative moment was moved to the right towards the mid span, while for
decreasing values of the horizontal reaction the critical point for negative moment was moved
to the left towards the support. It was therefore di’¼ācult to predict the location of the failure
on the non loaded side.
Another failure that occurred for some of the bridges was compression failure between the SL
elements at the non loaded side. For Bridge 1, 3 and 4 compression failure between the SL decks
occurred in either the 1/4 or 3/8 point according to the locations of the cracks. For Bridge
4 the compression failure was most likely caused by the deformed shape of the arch shown in
’¼üg. 9.26, which also caused an additional compression failure at the support at the non loaded
side. As seen compression failure did not occur for all the bridges, but only for Bridge 1 and 3,
which were the bridges with the low height of the ’¼ülling.
In order to obtain compression failure between the SL elements on the non loaded side for all
the bridges it could have been tried to increase the post tensioning of the arch. By looking
at the hand calculations for the theoretical bearing capacity, it was seen that increasing of the
post tensioning, would also cause increasing bearing capacity of the bridges. As mentioned in
section 6 a washer with a diameter of 30 mm was transferring the tension from the wire to the
arch as a compression force. Which meant that the force was applied as a concentrated load and
not as a distributed load over the entire cross section of the SL deck. Since the force was applied
as a point load, it was not possible to increase the post tensioning, because it could cause failure
or cracks in the arch before testing. If the post tensioning force should have been increased in
order to obtain a larger capacity and a certain type of failure, a steel plate with dimensions
equal to the cross section should have been used instead. But as mentioned previously this
knowledge was ’¼ürst brought to attention after the supporting beam had been produced and it
was therefore not possible to change.
As mentioned earlier the value of the post tensioning was measured with a tensioning devise
as illustrated in section 6. Since two bridges were constructed and tested at the same time
3 rings were used to measure the force. These rings were all calibrated in order to obtain a
correlation between signal and force for each of the rings. But at some point during the post
tensioning, it was noticed that the correlation between the signal and load, was no longer valid.
An explanation for this could be that when studying the normal forces in the wires during test
A of Bridge 1, it was seen that the force in the wire in the arch reached approximately 2300 N,
which was the maximum force the rings were calibrated for. If the maximum calibrated force
was exceeded, the correlation between signal and load would no longer be valid, meaning that
the calibration would have to be repeated in order to obtain a new valid correlation.
When post tensioning the following bridges it was seen that even when a signal corresponding to
0 N was expected, a signal of 300 N could be obtained. With that in mind, it was possible that
the maximum calibrated load was exceeded during the ’¼ürst test. Thus the results of the post
tensioning in the following tests were very uncertain and it was therefore di’¼ācult to evaluate
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
81
==PAGE:94==
10
Discussion
the e’¼Ćect of the post tensioning magnitude. In order to obtain more accurate results of the
tensile force the tensioning device should have been calibrated before every test or alternatively
a new type of tensioning device should have been developed.
When looking at the locations of the cracks in each of the bridges it was seen from ’¼üg. 9.19
and ’¼üg. 9.27 that for Bridge 1 test B and Bridge 4 failure occurred in the 3/8 point on the
non loaded side and at the 5/8 point at the loaded side. For Bridge 3B and Bridge 2 the main
cracks occurred at the 1/4 point at the non loaded side and likewise at the 3/4 point at the
loaded side according to ’¼üg. 9.24 and ’¼üg. 9.21 respectively. And for both bridges smaller cracks
also occurred at the 3/8 point and the 5/8 point. As mentioned earlier that cracks were placed
asymmetric on Bridge 3 according to asymmetry of the bridge, and therefore no further studies
were performed for Bridge 3. As mentioned previously Bridge 1 and 2 were constructed with the
weak gypsum as ’¼ülling while Bridge 3B and 4 were constructed with the strong gypsum ’¼ülling.
From the above mentioned it was hereby seen that the location of failure had no connection to
the material properties of the ’¼ülling. It was also studied if varying the height of the ’¼ülling had
an impact of the locations for failure. As mentioned previously Bridge 1 test B and Bridge 3
were constructed with connection between arch and top plate at the crown, while Bridge 2 and
4 had 20 mm extra height of the ’¼ülling. Again it was seen that no connection between cracks
and height of ’¼ülling was indicated. This indicates that the location of failure appeared to be
relatively random.
An explanation for the random location of failure could be discovered from the pictures showing
deformations and cracks in section 9. Here it is shown that the cracks in general started or
ended in the ’¼ülling just above the joints between the SL elements. This indicated that the joints
between the SL elements were crucial for the locations of the cracks. As discussed previously
the joints between the elements in the scaled models were not connected by a high strength
mortar like in the prototype model, and the cracks were therefore controlled by the weak point
in the joints. Due to the small scaling factor it would be a di’¼ācult and very time consuming
process to cast the joints and retain the keep the shape of the arch.
Another explanation for the random behaviour of failure is illustrated in table 5.1 showing the
variation of ’¼ülling height. It was clear that the height of the ’¼ülling varied a little from one bridge
to another, which might have had an e’¼Ćect on the location of the ’¼ürst cracks. As mentioned
before the angle at the ends of the SL elements were very important, and had a great e’¼Ćect on
the ’¼ünal dimensions of the bridge. And due to the materials and tools available for this project
it was not been possible to create the elements with a larger accuracy.
10.6
Color plots from ARAMIS
In section 9 a number of color plots are shown for each of the bridges in order to study the
most critical areas.
In general it was seen that the attachment between the materials was
not su’¼ācient. For all of the bridges tension occurred in the joints between arch and ’¼ülling
at the support at the non loaded side. As mentioned earlier the attachment between gypsum
and mortar was studied where it was clear that gypsum could not attach direct on the mortar
surface. Therefore several di’¼Ćerent methods were studied, for Bridge 1 and 2 sand was sprinkled
on the newly cast mortar or gypsum to create a more rough surface. After only a few seconds of
testing of Bridge 1 test A, splitting in the joint between arch and ’¼ülling occurred at the support
on the non loaded side as shown in ’¼üg. 9.17. It was hereby clear that using sand to roughen
the surface in order to obtain su’¼ācient attachment between the materials was not an option.
82
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:95==
10
Discussion
Therefore paper clips were placed between arch and ’¼ülling and between ’¼ülling and top plate for
Bridge 3, 3B and 4. By studying the color plots for Bridge 3 to 4 tension and hereby cracks
were still obtained at the support on the non loaded side, but no split occurred as it was the
case for Bridge 1 test A.
In general for all the bridges it was also seen that the attachment in the joints between both top
plate and ’¼ülling and arch and ’¼ülling were subjected to tension at the locations for the vertical
cracks. From the color plots in section 9 it is seen that all cracks occurred in the area from the
1/4 point to the 3/4 point, which resulted in critical joints within this area as illustrated in the
color plots.
These di’¼āculties about tension in the joints indicated that the paper clips was su’¼ācient to
prevent actually split between the materials as seen for Bridge 1 test A, but it was not su’¼ā-
cient enough to prevent cracks in the joints. In order to prevent both split and cracks in the
joints a more detailed study of the attachments between the chosen materials should have been
developed. As an example more paper clips could have been placed. Another solution could
be use a combination of the tested methods, where as an example sand and paper clips could
have been tests as one of the combinations, but due to the time limit of this project it was not
possible to perform these mentioned tests.
Another general behaviour observed from the color plots in section 9 was tension in the joints
between the SL elements at the 5/8 point and the 3/4 point on the loaded side. As mentioned
previously high strength mortar was used in the joints on the prototype bridge, but due to the
small geometry for the scaled models it was decided not to use any attachments between the
SL elements. The elements were hereby only connected due to the post tensioning from the
wire, which resulted in weaker joints. This behaviour was seen for the Simple Bridge, where no
cracks occurred but only splitting in the joints between the elements occurred, this indicated
that the joints were acting like cracks that had already occurred at these locations before the
actual test.
In order to obtain a behaviour more similar to the behaviour of the prototype bridge the SL
elements could have been connected by mortar. But as mentioned earlier it would be a di’¼ācult
task for the small scaled bridge.

==PAGE:96==

Discussion
10.7
Stresses from ARAMIS
Since the bridges were loaded with a point load in the 3/4 point, it would be expected that on
the loaded side tensile stresses would occur in the bottom and compressive stresses would occur
in the top of the bridge and opposite on the non-loaded side.
When studying the results of the normal strains in the top plate and the arch for the tested
bridges, it was seen that no general distribution of tension and compression was obtained. For
Bridge 2 and Bridge 4 the stresses in the arch were reasonable compared to what would have
been expected. But in the top plate for the same bridges the stresses were completely opposite of
what was expected. For the remaining bridges the stress distribution appeared to be relatively
random.
One factor that might have had an in’¼éuenced of the results was the position of the measurement.
As mentioned previously the theoretical calculations showed that the compressive zone would
correspond to about 1 mm in either the upper part of the top plate or the bottom of the
arch, depending on the operational sign of the bending moment. If the theoretical calculations
were valid, it would have ment that in order to monitor the compressive stresses, the strain
measurements should have been applied within 1 mm from the edge of the element. However
as seen in ’¼üg. 10.3 the ARAMIS software did not provide measurements at the edges of the
elements, and the strains in the outer facets were not accurate and should have been omitted
when calculating the strains.
Figure 10.3: Strains at edges of element.
Another factor that might had an e’¼Ćect on the results was the mutual distance between the
de’¼üned points in which the strains were measured. If the strains was measured over a too long
distance it would not represent the actual strains in the points within the measured length,
but instead a mean value of the strain over the entire length. In other words, if both tensile
stresses and compressive stresses occurred within the measured length, the results might ended
up incorrectly showing that no strains occurred.
If, on the other hand the strains were measured within small intervals, it would increase the
number of measurements, which took a lot of time to processed. It was therefore desired to
measure the strains over a length as long as possible, where reasonable results of the strains
was still obtained.
In order to check if the intervals applied for determining the normal strains were suitable the
left side of the top plate in Bridge 4 was studied. Originally the top plate was divided into 4
parts, all subjected to compression. The 4 parts were referred to as 1 to 4 as shown in ’¼üg. 10.4.
Each part was then separated into additional two parts referred to as a and b.
84
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:97==
10
Discussion
Figure 10.4: Length of strain measurements in top plate.
From the results in appendix J.3 it was seen that reducing the length from about 75 mm to about
35 mm did not had any signi’¼ücant e’¼Ćect on the strain. Afterwards 8 additional measurements
were conducted within part 1a. The measurements all had the same starting point but with
varying length as shown in ’¼üg. 10.5. Here it was found that the strain changed from compression
to tension when the length was reduced from 4.5 mm to 3 mm.
Figure 10.5: Length of strain measurements in top plate.
Finally, it was studied whether the results of the strains in the top plate was change if intervals
of 3 mm were de’¼üned. But as showed in the calculations the strains obtained with the 3 mm
intervals did not varied signi’¼ücantly from the strains obtained with the 75 mm intervals. Hence
it was assumed that the lengths that was applied for the measurements of the strains in the top
plate and the arch were su’¼ācient to obtain reasonable results.
As mentioned previously in the report the inaccuracies of the ARAMIS cameras was studied.
It was seen that the inaccuracy was larger for the 2D system than for the 3D system. A reason
for this could have been that the 2D pictures were taken by manually pushing down the trigger
on the camera, which might had caused some smaller movements of the camera. If the camera
was moved parallel to the measured surface it would not be expected to have an impact on the
strains, since the points would all be displaced in the same direction, and the mutual distance
between them would be the same. If, on the other hand the camera was turned or moved closer
to or further away from the measured surface, it would be expected to have an e’¼Ćect on the
strain measurements, since the mutual distance between the points would then be changed.
Regardless of the reason for the inaccuracy, it could have cause incorrect results. The inaccu-
racy was about 0.3% for the 2D system and 0.04% for the 3D system. Comparing these values
to the strain measurements it was seen that a large part of the calculated normal strains for the
bridges were smaller than 0.3% or 0.04%, meaning that there was great uncertainties regarding
these results.
The ARAMIS software was also applied in order to evaluate the stress distribution in the ’¼ülling
material. As mentioned previously the ’¼ülled spandrel arch bridges were assumed to behave as
a sandwich structure. But due to the varied height it was di’¼ācult to predict the behaviour
during loading.
When studying the results of strains obtained with ARAMIS it was di’¼ācult to see any general
tendencies of how the stresses were distributed within the ’¼ülling. In order to evaluate the results
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
85
==PAGE:98==
10
Discussion
the area under the loading point i.e. around base point C1 was studied. In this area it was
expected that compressive stresses occurred in the vertical direction due to the point load acted
right above it. For most of the bridges it was the case, but for Bridge 1 test B on the other hand
tensile stresses were monitored in the vertical direction. When Bridge 1 test B was conducted
the bridge had already been tested to failure once during test A as described previously. This
might have caused irregular behaviour of the bridge and di’¼Ćerent distribution of stresses during
the second loading.
For the measurements around the remaining base points i.e.
C2 to C5B it was di’¼ācult to
identify any consistencies in the stress distribution. But as it appeared from the test matrix in
table 1.1 a parameter was changed in each bridge either the height or the sti’¼Ćness of the ’¼ülling
material and only one bridge of each type were tested. And since no knowledge of the e’¼Ćect of
changing the di’¼Ćerent parameters were obtained. It was therefore di’¼ācult to evaluate whether
or not the measured strains represented the actual strains in the bridge, or if any potential
interfering factors had a’¼Ćected the results. In order to evaluate the results it would have been
bene’¼ücial to run a number of identical tests, where no parameters were changed. The strains
in the bridges would then have been expected to be more or less identical, and it would have
been easier to identify if some of the results was stand out.
Assuming that general tendencies in the behaviour would apply even when the parameters were
changed, interfering factors must had a’¼Ćected the results.
One factor could have been the measuring distance, which was also discussed previously for the
normal strains in the top plate and the arch. Here it was found that the strains where relatively
constant over longer distances, meaning that changing the measuring distance from 3 mm to
75 mm did not have signi’¼ücant e’¼Ćect on the strains.
By studying ’¼üg. 10.6 where the major strains of Bridge 4 captured with ARAMIS 2D is plotted,
it was indicated that the strains in the ’¼ülling material varied a lot over relatively short distances.
Figure 10.6: Strains in ’¼ülling material of Bridge 4.
Also, by studying the strains around base point C4 for Bridge 2 in appendix J.2, it was seen that
strain measurements resulted in both compression and tension in the vertical direction within a
relatively short distance. This observation con’¼ürmed the assumption of great strain variations
in the ’¼ülling material. If this was the case, then the measuring distance and the position of the
base point had great impact on the strain results.
For Bridge 2 the importance of the base point position was studied in appendix J.5, by comparing
the strains obtained for C1 to identical measurements for a point C1C placed approximately
10 mm to the right. In ’¼üg. 10.7 the results for both sets of measurements are shown. It was
seen that in both cases tension occurred in lines sloped downwards corresponding to about 45ŌŚ”
86
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:99==
10
Discussion
to the vertical axis. But for the strains perpendicular to this line and in the vertical direction
the two measurements showed di’¼Ćerent results. While C1C showed that compression occurred
in the vertical direction, C1 only 10 mm to the left showed that tension occurred in the same
direction.
Figure 10.7: Strains around C1 (to the left) and C1C (to the right) for Bridge 2.
Based on this knowledge it was found that the positions of the base points were extremely
important regarding the strain measurements. When the base points were de’¼üned in ARAMIS
they were placed as accurate as possibly allowed for the de’¼üned mesh, but deviations of about
5 mm would probably exist. The small deviation might leaded to completely di’¼Ćerent results
and could have been the reason for that no general tendencies was observed when the strains
in the ’¼ülling material was studied.
As already mentioned, the measuring distance did also had a great e’¼Ćect on the results. In
appendix J.4 the e’¼Ćect was studied for point C1 and C2 for Bridge 4. In both cases it was seen
that a change in the length from about 20 mm to 5 mm and from 10 mm to 5 mm respectively
the strains changed signi’¼ücantly, and in some directions was changed from tension to compres-
sion or the other way around, which con’¼ürmed that the length had great impact on the results.
Since the strains in the ’¼ülling material changed greatly within small intervals, it might have
been that the applied facet settings were too rough. If a smaller mesh was applied the results
would have been more accurate. But when decreasing the facet size, it might resulted in that
some areas of the surface could not be monitored.
If more time was available for the project, the e’¼Ćect of changing the facet size would be studied,
in order to see if it would have resulted in any tendencies for the strains in the ’¼ülling material.
Also it could have been that the ARAMIS software applied was not suitable for these kinds of
tests. In order to study a great part of the bridges, the large scale ARAMIS system was applied.
But maybe it would have been bene’¼ücial to use the small scale system where only smaller part
of the bridge could be focused. In this way the achieved results might have been more accurate.
Finally, another possibility could have been that the strains monitored in the ’¼ülling material
corresponded to the actual strains. And a reason for, what seemed to be a relatively random
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
87
==PAGE:100==
10
Discussion
stress distribution, could have been that the gypsum ’¼ülling was not homogeneous. When casting
the strong ’¼ülling material with w/g=0.25 the gypsum was not liquid and also it cured rapidly,
which might have caused holes in the ’¼ülling material. If this was the case, the stresses would
run completely di’¼Ćerent in the bridge.
10.8
Scaled model vs. Prototype
As mentioned previously the results obtained for the 1/12 scaled bridges could not be directly
transferred to the prototype bridge, due to numerous deviations. But instead they gave an
indication of what could possibly be expected for the behaviour of the prototype bridge.
Some of the deviations between the scaled model and the prototype was caused by the materials.
As mentioned in section 4 it had not been possible to ’¼ünd materials for the scaled bridge where
the ratio between strength and sti’¼Ćness corresponded to the ratio between strength and sti’¼Ćness
for the prototype bridge. This might had an e’¼Ćect on how the stresses were distributed within
the bridge.
When comparing the materials applied in the SL deck of the prototype bridge to the ones
applied in the scaled model, it was seen that there was a great di’¼Ćerence in the texture of
the light material. The light concrete had pores, which enabled the high strength concrete to
penetrate and hereby obtain optimal adherence between the materials. For the scaled bridge
the materials were cast in the opposite order. But both the gypsum and the mortar had smooth
surfaces, meaning that in order to obtain adherence between the two materials paper clips were
applied.
The same situation occurred between the ’¼ülling material and the top plate, where paper clips
also were placed to connect the two materials. But as indicated in the color plots of the major
strains obtained with ARAMIS, great tensile stresses occurred in the joints during loading,
meaning that paper clips did not provide su’¼ācient strength to transfer the stresses between the
top plate and the ’¼ülling. In the prototype bridge the ’¼ülling material would have been a light
concrete material, as the one applied in SL deck. This means that the adherence between the
top plate and the ’¼ülling would have been stronger than for the scaled model, meaning that the
prototype would be better to transfer the stresses than the scaled model, which would have an
e’¼Ćect on the behaviour of the entire bridge.
Also, as mentioned previously the ’¼ülling material in the scaled bridge was rather lumpy and not
as liquid as the light concrete for the prototype bridge, which might have resulted in that the
’¼ülling material was not homogeneously distributed in the scaled bridges. Any potential holes or
any more or less compacted areas of the ’¼ülling material would have great impact on the stress
distribution.
88
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark
==PAGE:101==
11
Conclusions
11
Conclusions
The purpose of this thesis was to investigate the e’¼Ćect of ’¼ülling material in ’¼ülled spandrel arch
bridges. Therefore a number of scaled bridges were constructed according to scaling theory for
strength models. From the theory it was given that new materials were to be found for the
scaled models, and a lot of di’¼Ćerent materials were tested. It was found that this process was a
very comprehensive task, and therefore the chosen materials for the scaled models did not cor-
respond to the materials for the prototype bridge. According to the deviations of i.a. materials
between the prototype bridge and the scaled models the obtained results for behaviour could
not be directly transferred from the models to a full sized bridge. Instead the results were used
as an indication of what might be expected as possible behaviour for a full sized bridge.
As mentioned two parameters were changed for the ’¼ülling material in order to study the e’¼Ćect,
where heigh of ’¼ülling and sti’¼Ćness of ’¼ülling varied. The e’¼Ćect of the changed parameters was
studied by performing theoretical calculations for the bearing capacity for all cases. According
to the theoretical bearing capacity only the variation of the ’¼ülling height would have an e’¼Ćect
on the bearing capacity. But the test results showed that both the increased height and the
decreasing w/g ratio had a positive e’¼Ćect on the bearing capacity. It was seen that the variation
of the height had the greatest e’¼Ćect. As mentioned the e’¼Ćect of post tensioning the top plate
was studied. The post tensioning of top plate showed to have a positive e’¼Ćect on the theoretical
bearing capacity, but this was not seen from the tests due to a construction error on one of the
bridges. In general it was shown that the maximum applied load during the tests was more
than four times the capacity obtained from the theoretical calculations. In order to develop the
theoretical calculations a number of conservative assumptions were used, in order to obtain a
lower bound solution. It was therefore expected that the capacity reached in the tests exceeded
the theoretical capacities.
During the tests, three measuring systems were used in order to obtain results for displacements
and stresses. When studying the measurements obtained with ARAMIS it was seen that the
results for displacements corresponded to displacements measured with LVDTs. As expected
the deformations of the bridges were obtaining an S shape, where the loaded side was moved
down and the non loaded side moved upwards. As assumed the greatest displacements occurred
in the y-direction, and smaller values in the x-direction, while the bridges had a signi’¼ücantly
small displacements in the z-direction. On the other hand the ARAMIS results for strains were
more inaccurate and it was therefore di’¼ācult to draw conclusions about how the strains were
distributed in the ’¼ülling material. In the meantime the color plots from ARAMIS could be used
to illustrate the critical areas of the bridges during testing. Here it was seen that the joints
between top plate and ’¼ülling and between arch and ’¼ülling were critical for all bridges and the
adherence between the materials was therefore not su’¼ācient. In the color plots it was seen
that stresses were transferred through the ’¼ülling material as shear stresses. It could therefore
indicate that the assumption about studying the bridge as a sandwich structure was valid.
All together the project showed that the results was inaccurate and no tendencies were observed
regarding the stress distribution. In order to con’¼ürm or deny if the obtained results for stresses
were valid more identical tests should have been developed. Also, in order to transfer the be-
haviour seen on the scaled models to a full sized bridge, some adjustments should have been
done. The fact that the joints between the SL decks were not cast, the alternative materials
and the simpli’¼üed design would be assumed to have an impact on the results.
Department of Civil Engineering - Technical University of Denmark

==PAGE:103==
12
Future Studies
12
Future Studies
During the progress of the project the attention was led to several subjects that could have
been improved or process di’¼Ćerently in order to obtain more accurate results. Also, as more
knowledge was achieved, new issues arose. Issues that due to the limited time frame have not
been processed in this project, but might give rise to new projects and future studies.
As already mentioned there were uncertainties about how to interpret the strain results obtained
with the ARAMIS software. In order to utilise the full potential of the software for future tests,
it could have been bene’¼ücial to apply ARAMIS on simple tensile of compressive tests where the
behaviour was more predictive. Additionally by applying strain gauges at the test specimen, it
would have been easier to evaluate the strain results obtained in ARAMIS.
In a future study it would be interesting to conduct more identical tests. This could for instance
be 5 tests of Bridge 1 and Bridge 2 respectively. In this way the e’¼Ćect of changing the height of
the ’¼ülling material could be further studied. If more identical tests were conducted, it would be
easier to identify if any results was standing out and if any potential tendencies occurred when
changing the height. The same would apply for Bridge 3 and Bridge 4 respectively in order to
study the e’¼Ćect of changing sti’¼Ćness of the ’¼ülling more detailed.
When studying the e’¼Ćect of changing the sti’¼Ćness of the ’¼ülling material it might also have
been interesting to study the case, where the ’¼ülling consisted of a material that was not able of
transferring any shear stresses. By studying a more extreme case, it would be expected to have
greater impact on the results. Also it would be interesting to see the in’¼éuence of the top plate
regarding the stress distribution.
Besides the already studied case where a point load was applied in the 3/4 point, it could be
interesting to study other load cases. For instance how the bridge would have behaved if it was
tested under a loading-unloading cycles.
For smaller arch bridges with a span of approximately 15 m or less, loading from tandem vehicles
is critical. It would therefore also be interesting to study the behaviour when the bridge was
subjected to load from tandem vehicles, which could be studied by summing two point loads of
55 tons with a mutual distance of 1 meter.
During the testing it was found that the applied attachment between gypsum and mortar did
not have su’¼ācient strength regarding the tension which occurred during loading. In a future
study other methods could be apply in order to prevent cracks and splitting in the joints espe-
cially at the support at the non-loaded side between arch and ’¼ülling.
Also it would be interesting to study how the bridges would behave during loading if the post
tensioning force in the arch and the top plate were increased. It would then be expected to see
more compressive failures of the mortar, which also was the case when testing the prototype
bridge.
Finally when a su’¼ācient number of scaled model tests had been conducted, and a potential
tendency of the behaviour was detected, it would be interesting to run a similar test on a real
scaled bridge in order to study if any of the tendencies could be expected to apply here as well.

Recipe for Salt Dough
D
Recipe for Salt Dough
Recipe for one portion of Salt Dough:
ŌĆó 3 dl Flour
ŌĆó 1 dl Salt
ŌĆó 2 tsp. Oil
ŌĆó 1-1.5 dl Water
Mix ’¼éour and salt.
Add Oil and stir.
Add water to the desired texture is obtained.

FORARBEJDNING
UNDERGRUND: Reng├Ėres grundigt ind til sund beton. Alle l├Ėse og
vedh├”ftningsh├”mmende dele s├źvel som cementslam fjernes ved
slyngrensning, sandbl├”sning eller h├Ėjtryksspuling. Undergrunden
skal have tilstr├”kkelig aftr├”ksstyrke (1,5 N/mm2). Ca. 6 timer f├Ėr
st├Ėbning forvandes til m├”tning. Forvandingen m├ź ikke efterlade en
d├”kkende vandfilm p├ź overfladen. St├Ėbningen udf├Ėres p├ź den mat-
fugtige undergrund.
FORSKALLING: F├”stnes godt og solidt. T├”tnes imod undergrunden
med sand eller t├Ėr m├Ėrtel.
BLANDING: M├Ėrtlen er brugsf├”rdig og skal kun blandes med rent
vand. Det meste af vandm├”ngden h├”ldes i en egnet blander (f.eks.
tvangsblander), m├Ėrtlen tilf├Ėres og der blandes ca. 2 minutter. Res-
ten af vandet tilf├Ėres, og der blandes yderligere ca. 1 minut, indtil
m├Ėrtlen er homogen. Forarbejdningen f├Ėlger straks herefter.
ST├śBNING: St├Ėbningen udf├Ėres fra ├®n side eller ├®t hj├Ėrne uden 
afbrydelser. Ved st├Ėrre opgaver anbefales det at benytte pumpe 
eller at st├Ėbe gennem tragt og slange - om muligt fra plademidten.
V├”r opm├”rksom p├ź forarbejdningstiden. (Beton-)undergrunden
skal v├”re frostfri.
BEM├åRK: Lave temperaturer og koldt blandevand kan forsinke
styrkeudviklingen, kr├”ver en mere intensiv blanding og reducerer
flydeevnen. H├Ėjere temperaturer forcerer styrkeudviklingen.
EFTERBEHANDLING: Fladerne skal straks efter st├Ėbning - senest
efter stivning af overfladen - beskyttes mod vind, tr├”k og for hurtig
fordampning i et tidsrum p├ź 3 - 5 dage, afh├”ngigt af undergrundens
og omgivelsernes temperatur.
Egnet efterbehandling er vandt├źge, og afd├”kning med eksempelvis
jute eller plast. 
Gr├”nsetemperaturer: +1 oC til +30 oC.
Frostbestandig efter 2 timer.
├ådelmetaller: Cement og cementbaserede materialer kan i kontakt-
omr├źder for├źrsage et nedbrydende angreb p├ź armering af metal 
(ikke jern, men for eksempel aluminium, kobber og zink). Kontakt os
for teknisk r├źdgivning.
